--- Lesson 3.18: Electron Affinity (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Electron Affinity
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is electron affinity?
Electron affinity is the energy change that occurs when a neutral gaseous atom gains an electron. A negative value means energy is released, indicating the atom readily accepts electrons.
What is an anion?
An anion is a negatively charged ion created when a neutral atom gains one or more electrons. Elements with high electron affinity readily form anions.
What is a halide ion?
A halide ion is formed when a halogen gains one electron, giving it a -1 charge and a full outer shell. Examples include fluoride (F-), chloride (Cl-), and bromide (Br-).

--- Lesson 3.18: Electron Affinity (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What happens to electron affinity as you move from left to right across a period?
    [*] It generally becomes more negative (more energy released)
    [ ] It generally becomes less negative (less energy released)
    [ ] It stays exactly the same across the entire period
    [ ] It alternates between positive and negative values
    Explanation: Electron affinity generally becomes more negative across a period, meaning atoms release more energy when gaining an electron. This is because the increasing nuclear charge attracts additional electrons more strongly.

  Q2. Which group of elements generally has the most negative electron affinity (releases the most energy when gaining electrons)?
    [ ] Group 1, alkali metals
    [ ] Group 18, noble gases
    [*] Group 17, halogens
    [ ] Group 2, alkaline earth metals
    Explanation: Halogens (Group 17) have the most negative electron affinities because they need only one more electron to complete their octet, and gaining that electron releases a large amount of energy.

  Q3. What happens to electron affinity as you move down a group?
    [ ] It generally becomes more negative down the group
    [ ] It stays constant throughout the entire group column
    [*] It generally becomes less negative down the group
    [ ] It increases dramatically with each new period added
    Explanation: Electron affinity generally becomes less negative going down a group because larger atoms with more electron shielding are less effective at attracting an additional electron.

  Q4. Which of the following elements has the most negative electron affinity?
    [ ] Sodium
    [*] Chlorine
    [ ] Calcium
    [ ] Argon
    Explanation: Chlorine has the most negative electron affinity of all elements. It strongly attracts an additional electron because it needs just one more to complete its octet.

  Q5. Do noble gases have favorable electron affinities?
    [ ] Yes, they strongly attract extra electrons to their shells
    [*] No, their full outer shells resist gaining extra electrons
    [ ] Yes, but only the heavier noble gases like xenon and radon
    [ ] No, because noble gases do not have any electrons at all
    Explanation: Noble gases have unfavorable (positive or near-zero) electron affinities because their outer shells are already full. Adding an electron would go into a new, higher energy level, which is not energetically favorable.

  Q6. What sign is the electron affinity value when energy is released as an atom gains an electron?
    [ ] Positive, indicating energy is absorbed
    [*] Negative, indicating energy is released
    [ ] Zero, indicating no energy is exchanged
    [ ] It has no sign and is always unsigned
    Explanation: When energy is released as an atom gains an electron, the electron affinity is negative. A more negative value means the atom more strongly attracts electrons.

  Q7. Why do Group 2 elements (alkaline earth metals) have nearly zero or positive electron affinities?
    [*] They already have a full s sublevel which is relatively stable
    [ ] They have too many protons to attract any electrons at all
    [ ] They have no electrons in their outer shell to begin with
    [ ] They are metals and metals always have positive electron affinity
    Explanation: Group 2 elements have a full s sublevel (2 electrons in s orbital), which is relatively stable. Adding another electron would require starting a new, higher-energy p sublevel, which is not favorable.

  Q8. Is electron affinity the same thing as electronegativity?
    [ ] Yes, they are different names for the exact same property
    [*] No, electron affinity is for isolated atoms while electronegativity is within bonds
    [ ] Yes, but electron affinity only applies to metals while electronegativity applies to nonmetals
    [ ] No, electron affinity measures losing electrons while electronegativity measures gaining them
    Explanation: They are related but different. Electron affinity measures the energy change when an isolated gaseous atom gains an electron. Electronegativity measures how strongly an atom attracts shared electrons within a chemical bond.

  Q9. If an element has a very negative electron affinity, would it likely be a metal or nonmetal?
    [ ] A metal, because metals gain electrons easily
    [*] A nonmetal, because nonmetals tend to gain electrons
    [ ] A metalloid, because they have intermediate properties
    [ ] A noble gas, because they have full outer shells already
    Explanation: Elements with very negative electron affinities are typically nonmetals, especially halogens. They strongly attract electrons to complete their outer shells.

  Q10. How does electron affinity help predict what type of ion an element will form?
    [*] Elements with very negative electron affinity tend to form negative ions
    [ ] Elements with very negative electron affinity tend to form positive ions
    [ ] Electron affinity has no relation to what type of ion an element forms
    [ ] All elements form the same type of ion regardless of electron affinity
    Explanation: Elements with very negative electron affinities readily gain electrons, so they tend to form negative ions (anions). Halogens, with the most negative electron affinities, commonly form -1 ions.

  Q11. What is electron affinity?
    [*] The energy change when a gaseous atom gains an electron from its surroundings
    [ ] The energy needed to remove an electron from a neutral gaseous atom completely
    [ ] The tendency of an atom to attract shared electrons in a chemical bond formed
    [ ] The distance from the nucleus to the outermost electron in a neutral atom today
    Explanation: Electron affinity is the energy change that occurs when a neutral gaseous atom gains an electron. A negative value means energy is released, indicating the atom readily accepts electrons.

  Q12. What does 'exothermic' mean when discussing electron affinity?
    [*] A process that releases energy to the surroundings as heat or light
    [ ] A process that absorbs energy from the surroundings during the reaction
    [ ] A process that occurs only at extremely high temperatures in a laboratory
    [ ] A process that does not involve any exchange of energy with surroundings
    Explanation: An exothermic process releases energy. When an atom with negative electron affinity gains an electron, energy is released because the atom becomes more stable.

  Q13. What does 'endothermic' mean when discussing electron affinity?
    [*] A process that requires energy input and absorbs energy from surroundings
    [ ] A process that releases energy and heat to the surrounding environment
    [ ] A process that only happens inside living organisms and biological cells
    [ ] A process that results in no change in energy for the atoms involved
    Explanation: An endothermic process requires energy input. Atoms with positive electron affinity (like noble gases) require energy to force them to accept an additional electron.

  Q14. What does 'negative electron affinity value' mean?
    [*] The atom releases energy when it gains an electron becoming more stable
    [ ] The atom loses energy levels when an electron is added to its outer shell
    [ ] The atom has fewer electrons than protons in its neutral atomic state today
    [ ] The atom cannot gain any electrons under any conditions or circumstances
    Explanation: A negative electron affinity value means the process of gaining an electron is exothermic -- energy is released. The more negative the value, the more strongly the atom attracts electrons.

  Q15. What is an anion?
    [*] A negatively charged ion formed when an atom gains one or more electrons
    [ ] A positively charged ion formed when an atom loses one or more electrons
    [ ] A neutral atom that has equal numbers of protons and electrons in it
    [ ] A particle found inside the nucleus alongside the protons and neutrons
    Explanation: An anion is a negatively charged ion created when a neutral atom gains one or more electrons. Elements with high electron affinity readily form anions.

  Q16. What does 'electron configuration stability' mean?
    [*] How energetically favorable an atom's electron arrangement is overall
    [ ] The physical strength of the bonds holding electrons in their orbitals
    [ ] The speed at which electrons move around the nucleus of a single atom
    [ ] The number of electrons that can fit in the first energy level of any atom
    Explanation: Electron configuration stability refers to how energetically favorable an atom's arrangement of electrons is. Full shells, half-full subshells, and completed octets are especially stable configurations.

  Q17. What does 'kilojoules per mole' mean as a unit for electron affinity?
    [*] The energy in thousands of joules exchanged per mole of atoms gaining electrons
    [ ] The number of moles of electrons that can be added to one kilogram of atoms
    [ ] The mass of electrons in kilograms that one mole of atoms contains in total
    [ ] The temperature in degrees at which one mole of atoms begins gaining electrons
    Explanation: Kilojoules per mole (kJ/mol) measures the energy change in thousands of joules when one mole of gaseous atoms each gain one electron.

  Q18. What does 'electron gain' mean?
    [*] The process of an atom accepting an additional electron into its structure
    [ ] The process of an atom releasing an electron from its outermost shell
    [ ] The increase in size of an electron as it moves to a higher energy level
    [ ] The movement of an electron between two bonded atoms in a molecule made
    Explanation: Electron gain is the process of a neutral atom accepting an additional electron, which changes it into a negatively charged anion. Electron affinity measures the energy associated with this process.

  Q19. What is a halide ion?
    [*] A halogen atom that has gained one electron to form a -1 charged ion
    [ ] A halogen atom that has lost one electron to form a +1 charged ion
    [ ] A halogen atom in its neutral state before any reaction has occurred
    [ ] Two halogen atoms bonded together forming a diatomic molecule pair
    Explanation: A halide ion is formed when a halogen gains one electron, giving it a -1 charge and a full outer shell. Examples include fluoride (F-), chloride (Cl-), and bromide (Br-).

  Q20. What does 'periodic trend in electron affinity' mean?
    [*] The predictable pattern of electron affinity values across periods and down groups
    [ ] The random changes in electron affinity that cannot be predicted by position
    [ ] The constant electron affinity value shared by all elements in the same period
    [ ] The change in electron affinity caused by changing temperature in a laboratory
    Explanation: The periodic trend in electron affinity describes the predictable pattern: electron affinity generally becomes more negative across a period and less negative down a group.

  Q21. Chlorine has an electron affinity of -349 kJ/mol and sodium has -53 kJ/mol. Which element more readily gains an electron?
    [ ] Sodium, because its electron affinity value is closer to zero overall
    [*] Chlorine, because its more negative value means more energy is released
    [ ] Both gain electrons equally well since they are in the same period row
    [ ] Neither gains electrons because both values are negative numbers here
    Explanation: Chlorine more readily gains an electron. Its electron affinity of -349 kJ/mol means much more energy is released when it gains an electron compared to sodium's -53 kJ/mol.

  Q22. A student notices that nitrogen has a less negative electron affinity than carbon, even though nitrogen is further right in the period. Why does this exception occur?
    [*] Nitrogen has a stable half-filled p sublevel that resists gaining another electron
    [ ] Nitrogen has fewer protons than carbon which makes it attract electrons less well
    [ ] Carbon is actually to the right of nitrogen on the periodic table not to the left
    [ ] The trend always goes in the opposite direction for nonmetals in the second period
    Explanation: Nitrogen has a half-filled p sublevel (one electron in each of the three p orbitals), which is especially stable. Adding an electron would require pairing, which is energetically less favorable.

  Q23. Neon has a positive electron affinity, meaning energy must be added to force it to accept an electron. Why?
    [*] Neon already has a completely full outer shell so adding an electron is unfavorable
    [ ] Neon has no electrons at all so there is nothing to attract another electron to it
    [ ] Neon is too small for any additional electrons to physically fit around its nucleus
    [ ] Neon is a metal and all metals resist gaining electrons due to their electron structure
    Explanation: Neon's outer shell is completely full with 8 electrons. An extra electron would have to go into a new, higher energy level far from the nucleus, which requires energy input rather than releasing it.

  Q24. A scientist is designing a battery and needs an element that strongly attracts electrons. Based on electron affinity trends, which area of the periodic table should they look?
    [ ] Bottom left, where the alkali metals are located on the periodic table
    [*] Upper right, where the halogens and reactive nonmetals are on the table
    [ ] Far right column, where the noble gases are located on the periodic table
    [ ] Center of the table, where the transition metals are located in the table
    Explanation: Elements in the upper right of the periodic table (excluding noble gases) have the most negative electron affinities. Halogens like fluorine and chlorine are the strongest electron attractors.

  Q25. Fluorine has a less negative electron affinity than chlorine (-328 vs -349 kJ/mol), even though fluorine is higher in Group 17. Why?
    [*] Fluorine is so small that its electrons repel an incoming electron in the tiny space
    [ ] Fluorine has fewer protons than chlorine so it cannot attract electrons as strongly
    [ ] Chlorine is a gas at room temperature while fluorine is a solid making it different
    [ ] The measurement for fluorine is incorrect and should actually be more negative here
    Explanation: Fluorine is so small that its existing 7 electrons are packed tightly together, creating strong electron-electron repulsion. This makes it slightly harder for an incoming electron to join, despite fluorine's high nuclear charge.

  Q26. Lithium has an electron affinity of -60 kJ/mol. Would lithium be more likely to gain or lose an electron in chemical reactions?
    [ ] Gain an electron, because its electron affinity is negative in value
    [*] Lose an electron, because its ionization energy is much lower than its electron affinity
    [ ] Neither, because lithium is completely unreactive like a noble gas element
    [ ] Both equally, because metals can gain and lose electrons at the same rates
    Explanation: Although lithium has a slightly negative electron affinity, it much more readily loses its single valence electron (low ionization energy). Metals like lithium form positive ions, not negative ones.

  Q27. An element has a very negative electron affinity and high electronegativity. It forms a -1 ion easily. Which group is it most likely in?
    [ ] Group 1, the alkali metals family
    [ ] Group 2, the alkaline earth metals
    [*] Group 17, the halogens family
    [ ] Group 18, the noble gases family
    Explanation: Very negative electron affinity, high electronegativity, and forming -1 ions are all characteristic of halogens (Group 17). They need one electron to complete their octet.

  Q28. Beryllium has a positive electron affinity. A student predicts magnesium (below beryllium in Group 2) also has a positive or near-zero electron affinity. Is this a good prediction?
    [ ] No, magnesium should have very negative electron affinity because it is larger
    [*] Yes, Group 2 elements have full s sublevels making electron gain unfavorable
    [ ] No, elements lower in a group always have more negative electron affinity
    [ ] Yes, but only because magnesium has the same number of valence electrons
    Explanation: This is a good prediction. All Group 2 elements have full s sublevels, making them relatively resistant to gaining additional electrons. Their electron affinities are positive or near zero.

  Q29. Oxygen has an electron affinity of -141 kJ/mol for gaining its first electron. However, its second electron affinity is positive (+744 kJ/mol). Why is gaining the second electron so unfavorable?
    [*] Adding a second electron to an already negative ion requires overcoming repulsion
    [ ] Oxygen can only hold one extra electron in its outer shell before it becomes full
    [ ] The second electron goes into the nucleus instead of the electron cloud around it
    [ ] Oxygen changes into a different element after gaining its first extra electron here
    Explanation: After gaining one electron, oxygen becomes O-. Adding a second electron means forcing a negative electron onto an already negatively charged ion, which requires significant energy to overcome the repulsion.

  Q30. A student compares the electron affinities of elements across Period 3. They notice the general trend but see that sodium's value (-53 kJ/mol) is less negative than expected. What might explain this?
    [ ] Sodium would need to start filling a new sublevel which is not as favorable for it
    [*] Sodium has only one valence electron and gaining one does not complete a stable shell
    [ ] Sodium is actually a noble gas and noble gases do not have favorable electron affinity
    [ ] The sodium value is incorrect and should be recalculated with better lab equipment used
    Explanation: Sodium has 1 valence electron. Gaining one more would give it only 2 valence electrons, which does not achieve a stable octet. Since gaining an electron does not greatly increase stability, the energy released is small.

--- Lesson 3.19: Lewis Dot Structures (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Lewis Dot Structures
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is the maximum number of dots that can surround an element symbol in a Lewis structure?
The maximum is 8 dots, representing 8 valence electrons (a complete octet). This is consistent with the octet rule for main group elements.
What is a Lewis dot structure?
A Lewis dot structure is a simple diagram that shows an element's chemical symbol surrounded by dots representing its valence electrons. It was developed by Gilbert N. Lewis.
What is the octet rule?
The octet rule states atoms tend to form bonds so they have 8 valence electrons (like noble gases). Hydrogen is an exception, needing only 2 electrons (a duet).
What is a single bond?
A single bond is formed when two atoms share one pair of electrons (2 electrons total). It is represented by a single line between atoms in Lewis structures.

--- Lesson 3.19: Lewis Dot Structures (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What do the dots in a Lewis dot structure represent?
    [ ] All electrons in the atom
    [*] Only the valence electrons
    [ ] Only the protons in the atom
    [ ] The neutrons in the nucleus
    Explanation: In a Lewis dot structure, the dots represent only the valence electrons (outermost electrons) of an atom. These are the electrons involved in chemical bonding.

  Q2. How many dots would a Lewis dot structure for carbon have?
    [ ] 2 dots
    [ ] 6 dots
    [*] 4 dots
    [ ] 8 dots
    Explanation: Carbon is in Group 14 and has 4 valence electrons, so its Lewis dot structure has 4 dots around the chemical symbol C.

  Q3. How many dots would a Lewis dot structure for neon have?
    [ ] 2 dots
    [ ] 10 dots
    [*] 8 dots
    [ ] 0 dots
    Explanation: Neon is in Group 18 and has 8 valence electrons. Its Lewis dot structure has 8 dots (4 pairs) around the symbol Ne, showing a complete outer shell.

  Q4. Where are the dots placed around the chemical symbol in a Lewis structure?
    [ ] All dots go on one side of the symbol only
    [*] On the top, bottom, left, and right sides
    [ ] In a straight line above the element symbol
    [ ] Inside the element symbol between the letters
    Explanation: Dots are placed on four sides of the element symbol (top, bottom, left, right). Each side can hold up to 2 dots, for a maximum of 8 valence electrons.

  Q5. How many dots would a Lewis dot structure for sodium (Na) have?
    [*] 1 dot
    [ ] 11 dots
    [ ] 2 dots
    [ ] 8 dots
    Explanation: Sodium is in Group 1 and has 1 valence electron. Its Lewis dot structure shows just 1 dot next to the Na symbol.

  Q6. How can you determine the number of valence electrons for a main group element?
    [*] By looking at the ones digit of the group number
    [ ] By counting the total number of protons in the atom
    [ ] By looking at the period number on the periodic table
    [ ] By dividing the atomic mass by the atomic number value
    Explanation: For main group elements, the ones digit of the group number tells you the number of valence electrons. Group 1 = 1, Group 2 = 2, Group 13 = 3, Group 14 = 4, and so on.

  Q7. How many dots would a Lewis dot structure for chlorine have?
    [ ] 17 dots total
    [*] 7 dots total
    [ ] 1 dot total
    [ ] 8 dots total
    Explanation: Chlorine is in Group 17 and has 7 valence electrons. Its Lewis dot structure has 7 dots around the Cl symbol, with three pairs and one unpaired electron.

  Q8. What is the maximum number of dots that can surround an element symbol in a Lewis structure?
    [ ] 4 dots maximum
    [ ] 6 dots maximum
    [*] 8 dots maximum
    [ ] 10 dots maximum
    Explanation: The maximum is 8 dots, representing 8 valence electrons (a complete octet). This is consistent with the octet rule for main group elements.

  Q9. When drawing Lewis dots, you should place one dot on each side before pairing. This follows which rule?
    [ ] The octet rule for electron shells
    [*] Hund's rule for electron distribution
    [ ] The periodic law for the elements listed
    [ ] Dalton's rule for atomic composition
    Explanation: This follows Hund's rule, which states electrons occupy empty orbitals singly before pairing up. In Lewis structures, place one dot on each of the four sides before adding second dots.

  Q10. How many dots would a Lewis dot structure for helium have?
    [ ] 8 dots
    [ ] 0 dots
    [*] 2 dots
    [ ] 4 dots
    Explanation: Helium is in Group 18 but only has 2 valence electrons (its first shell can only hold 2). Its Lewis structure shows 2 dots, representing a full first energy level.

  Q11. What is a Lewis dot structure?
    [*] A diagram showing an element's symbol surrounded by dots for valence electrons
    [ ] A diagram showing all the subatomic particles inside the nucleus of an atom
    [ ] A chart that lists every element and its position on the periodic table only
    [ ] A model that shows the three-dimensional shape of a complex molecule in space
    Explanation: A Lewis dot structure is a simple diagram that shows an element's chemical symbol surrounded by dots representing its valence electrons. It was developed by Gilbert N. Lewis.

  Q12. What does 'valence electron' mean?
    [*] An electron in the outermost energy level that participates in bonding
    [ ] Any electron found in the innermost energy level closest to the nucleus
    [ ] An electron that has been completely removed from an atom entirely
    [ ] A proton that orbits around the nucleus alongside the other electrons
    Explanation: A valence electron is an electron in the outermost energy level of an atom. Valence electrons are the ones involved in forming chemical bonds with other atoms.

  Q13. What does 'lone pair' mean in Lewis structures?
    [*] A pair of valence electrons that are not shared with another atom in bonds
    [ ] A pair of electrons shared between two atoms in a single covalent bond
    [ ] A pair of protons found inside the nucleus of a nonmetal element's atom
    [ ] A pair of atoms that are bonded together in a diatomic molecule formed
    Explanation: A lone pair (or nonbonding pair) is a pair of valence electrons on an atom that are not involved in bonding. They appear as paired dots on one side of the element symbol.

  Q14. What does 'bonding pair' mean?
    [*] A pair of electrons shared between two atoms to form a covalent bond
    [ ] Two atoms that happen to be next to each other on the periodic table
    [ ] A pair of protons that hold the nucleus of an atom together tightly
    [ ] Two lone pairs of electrons sitting on the same side of an atom now
    Explanation: A bonding pair is a pair of electrons that is shared between two atoms in a covalent bond. In Lewis structures, bonding pairs are shown as lines or shared dots between atoms.

  Q15. What is the octet rule?
    [*] Atoms tend to gain, lose, or share electrons to have 8 valence electrons
    [ ] Every Lewis structure must have exactly 8 dots around each element symbol
    [ ] Each element can only form a maximum of 8 chemical bonds with other atoms
    [ ] Atoms in the first period always have 8 electrons in their outer energy level
    Explanation: The octet rule states atoms tend to form bonds so they have 8 valence electrons (like noble gases). Hydrogen is an exception, needing only 2 electrons (a duet).

  Q16. What does 'duet rule' mean?
    [*] Hydrogen and helium are stable with just 2 electrons in their outer shell
    [ ] All atoms need exactly 2 electrons to be considered chemically stable
    [ ] Every bond must contain exactly 2 atoms bonded together in a pair formed
    [ ] Lewis structures must always show exactly 2 dots on each side of a symbol
    Explanation: The duet rule applies to hydrogen and helium, which need only 2 electrons to fill their first (and only) energy level. This is an exception to the octet rule.

  Q17. What does 'unpaired electron' mean?
    [*] A single valence electron that is not paired with another electron on that side
    [ ] An electron that has been removed from an atom during ionization process
    [ ] An electron that is shared between three or more atoms in a large molecule
    [ ] An electron found in the innermost shell of the atom near the nucleus core
    Explanation: An unpaired electron is a single electron that occupies an orbital by itself, without a partner. In Lewis structures, it appears as a single dot on one side of the element symbol.

  Q18. What does 'chemical symbol' mean in a Lewis dot structure?
    [*] The one or two letter abbreviation representing the element at the center
    [ ] The dots that represent the valence electrons surrounding the element shown
    [ ] The lines drawn between two atoms to represent shared electron pairs formed
    [ ] The number written below the element to show how many atoms are present
    Explanation: The chemical symbol is the one- or two-letter abbreviation for the element (like Na for sodium or O for oxygen). In Lewis structures, dots are placed around this symbol.

  Q19. What is a single bond?
    [*] A bond formed by the sharing of one pair of electrons between two atoms
    [ ] A bond where only one atom contributes electrons to the shared pair made
    [ ] A bond that can only form between a metal and a nonmetal element pair
    [ ] A bond that exists for only a single moment before breaking apart again
    Explanation: A single bond is formed when two atoms share one pair of electrons (2 electrons total). It is represented by a single line between atoms in Lewis structures.

  Q20. What does 'double bond' mean?
    [*] A bond formed by the sharing of two pairs of electrons between two atoms
    [ ] A bond that is twice as long as a normal single bond between two atoms
    [ ] A bond where each atom gives up two electrons to form positive ions here
    [ ] Two separate single bonds between the same pair of atoms in a molecule
    Explanation: A double bond involves sharing 4 electrons (2 pairs) between two atoms. It is represented by two parallel lines (=) and is shorter and stronger than a single bond.

  Q21. A student needs to draw the Lewis dot structure for oxygen (Group 16). How many dots should be placed around the O symbol?
    [ ] 8 dots, because oxygen needs a full octet to be stable
    [*] 6 dots, because Group 16 elements have 6 valence electrons
    [ ] 2 dots, because oxygen typically gains 2 electrons in bonding
    [ ] 16 dots, because oxygen is in Group 16 of the periodic table
    Explanation: Oxygen is in Group 16, so it has 6 valence electrons. The Lewis dot structure shows 6 dots around the O symbol: 2 lone pairs and 2 unpaired electrons available for bonding.

  Q22. A student draws the Lewis structure for water (H2O). Oxygen has 6 valence electrons and each hydrogen has 1. How many lone pairs does oxygen have in the completed structure?
    [ ] 0 lone pairs, because all electrons are used in bonding
    [ ] 1 lone pair on the oxygen atom in the water molecule
    [*] 2 lone pairs on the oxygen atom in the water molecule
    [ ] 4 lone pairs on the oxygen atom in the water molecule
    Explanation: In water, oxygen forms 2 single bonds with hydrogen (using 2 pairs of electrons for bonding) and retains 2 lone pairs. This gives oxygen 8 electrons total, satisfying the octet rule.

  Q23. Two nitrogen atoms bond together to form N2. Each nitrogen has 5 valence electrons. How many electron pairs are shared between the two nitrogen atoms?
    [ ] 1 shared pair, forming a single bond between the atoms
    [ ] 2 shared pairs, forming a double bond between the atoms
    [*] 3 shared pairs, forming a triple bond between the atoms
    [ ] 5 shared pairs, one for each valence electron on nitrogen
    Explanation: Each nitrogen needs 3 more electrons to complete its octet. By sharing 3 pairs of electrons (6 electrons total), both nitrogen atoms achieve 8 valence electrons. This creates a triple bond.

  Q24. A student draws a Lewis structure for methane (CH4). Carbon has 4 dots and each hydrogen has 1 dot. How many bonds does carbon form?
    [ ] 2 single bonds with 2 lone pairs remaining on carbon
    [*] 4 single bonds with no lone pairs remaining on carbon
    [ ] 1 single bond with 3 lone pairs remaining on carbon
    [ ] 3 single bonds with 1 lone pair remaining on carbon
    Explanation: Carbon has 4 valence electrons and shares one with each of 4 hydrogen atoms, forming 4 single bonds. This gives carbon 8 electrons (octet) and each hydrogen 2 electrons (duet).

  Q25. Fluorine (F2) exists as a diatomic molecule. Each fluorine has 7 valence electrons. In the Lewis structure of F2, how many lone pairs are on EACH fluorine atom?
    [ ] 1 lone pair on each fluorine atom in the molecule
    [ ] 2 lone pairs on each fluorine atom in the molecule
    [*] 3 lone pairs on each fluorine atom in the molecule
    [ ] 4 lone pairs on each fluorine atom in the molecule
    Explanation: Each fluorine shares 1 pair of electrons with the other (single bond), leaving 3 lone pairs on each atom. Each F has 2 bonding electrons + 6 lone pair electrons = 8 total, satisfying the octet rule.

  Q26. A student draws a Lewis structure for CO2 where each oxygen has a single bond to carbon. The student checks and finds carbon only has 6 electrons. What should the student do?
    [ ] Add more dots to carbon even though they do not come from any atom
    [*] Change the single bonds to double bonds so carbon gets a full octet
    [ ] Leave the structure as is because 6 electrons is acceptable for carbon
    [ ] Remove one oxygen from the structure so carbon can have fewer bonds
    Explanation: The student should change each C-O single bond to a C=O double bond. This gives carbon 8 electrons (4 from each double bond), and each oxygen also gets 8 electrons including lone pairs.

  Q27. Phosphorus (P) is in Group 15 and has 5 valence electrons. A student draws its Lewis dot structure with 3 unpaired electrons and 1 lone pair. Is this correct?
    [ ] No, phosphorus should have 5 unpaired electrons with no lone pairs at all
    [ ] No, phosphorus should have 5 lone pairs with no unpaired electrons at all
    [*] Yes, with 5 electrons there would be 3 unpaired and 1 lone pair of electrons
    [ ] Yes, but only if the lone pair is placed on the top side of the element symbol
    Explanation: This is correct. Following Hund's rule, place one dot on each of 4 sides first (using 4 electrons), then pair the 5th with one of the existing dots. This gives 3 single dots and 1 pair.

  Q28. In the Lewis structure for hydrogen chloride (HCl), hydrogen has 1 valence electron and chlorine has 7. After the bond forms, how many total electrons surround chlorine?
    [ ] 6 electrons surround chlorine in the completed structure
    [ ] 7 electrons surround chlorine in the completed structure
    [*] 8 electrons surround chlorine in the completed structure
    [ ] 2 electrons surround chlorine in the completed structure
    Explanation: Chlorine has 7 valence electrons and shares 1 with hydrogen. In the Lewis structure, chlorine has 2 bonding electrons + 6 lone pair electrons = 8 total electrons, satisfying the octet rule.

  Q29. A student notices that nitrogen in ammonia (NH3) has one lone pair while nitrogen in N2 has one lone pair as well. However, nitrogen forms 3 bonds in NH3 and a triple bond in N2. Why can nitrogen form different numbers of bonds?
    [*] Nitrogen always forms the number of bonds needed to complete its octet
    [ ] Nitrogen randomly changes how many bonds it forms in every molecule made
    [ ] Nitrogen can only form triple bonds because it has 3 unpaired electrons
    [ ] The number of bonds is determined by the mass of the atoms it bonds with
    Explanation: Nitrogen needs 3 more electrons for an octet. In NH3, it shares one electron with each of 3 hydrogens (3 single bonds). In N2, it shares 3 electrons with another nitrogen (1 triple bond). Both satisfy the octet.

  Q30. A student tries to draw a Lewis structure for NaCl. Should this structure show shared electrons between Na and Cl?
    [ ] Yes, all bonds involve shared electrons between the bonded atoms
    [*] No, NaCl is ionic so Na transfers its electron to Cl instead of sharing
    [ ] Yes, because sodium and chlorine both need to complete their octets
    [ ] No, because sodium has no valence electrons to share or transfer here
    Explanation: NaCl is an ionic compound. Sodium transfers its 1 valence electron to chlorine rather than sharing it. The Lewis structure shows Na+ (no dots) and Cl- (8 dots with brackets and a minus sign).

--- Lesson 3.20: Ionic vs. Covalent Bonding (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Ionic vs. Covalent Bonding
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is an ionic bond?
An ionic bond is the electrostatic attraction between oppositely charged ions formed when one atom transfers electrons to another. Typically, a metal loses electrons and a nonmetal gains them.
What is a covalent bond?
A covalent bond forms when two atoms share one or more pairs of electrons. Both atoms contribute electrons to the bond, and the shared electrons are attracted to both nuclei.
What is a molecule?
A molecule is a group of two or more atoms held together by covalent bonds. Molecules are the smallest unit of a covalent compound that retains its chemical properties.
What is a polyatomic ion?
A polyatomic ion is a group of atoms that are covalently bonded together but carry an overall charge. Examples include hydroxide (OH-), sulfate (SO4 2-), and ammonium (NH4+).
A student asks why water (a covalent compound) is liquid at room temperature while many other covalent compounds are gases. What is the best explanation?
Water is liquid at room temperature because of strong hydrogen bonds between water molecules. These intermolecular forces are stronger than those in most covalent compounds, keeping water in the liquid state.
Sodium has 1 valence electron and oxygen has 6. When they form an ionic compound, what is the correct formula?
Oxygen needs 2 electrons to complete its octet, but each sodium can only give 1. So 2 sodium atoms each transfer 1 electron to oxygen, making the formula Na2O.
Real-World Applications
Example: Which of the following is an example of an ionic compound?
Sodium chloride (NaCl) is ionic because sodium is a metal that transfers its electron to chlorine, a nonmetal. The other compounds listed are covalent because they involve only nonmetals.

--- Lesson 3.20: Ionic vs. Covalent Bonding (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What type of bond forms between a metal and a nonmetal?
    [ ] Covalent bond
    [ ] Metallic bond
    [*] Ionic bond
    [ ] Hydrogen bond
    Explanation: Ionic bonds form between metals and nonmetals. The metal transfers electrons to the nonmetal, creating oppositely charged ions that attract each other.

  Q2. What type of bond forms between two nonmetals?
    [ ] Ionic bond
    [ ] Metallic bond
    [*] Covalent bond
    [ ] Nuclear bond
    Explanation: Covalent bonds form between two nonmetals. Instead of transferring electrons, nonmetals share electrons to achieve a stable electron configuration.

  Q3. In an ionic bond, what happens to the electrons?
    [ ] Electrons are shared equally between the two atoms
    [*] Electrons are transferred from one atom to another
    [ ] Electrons are destroyed during the bonding process
    [ ] Electrons move into the nucleus of both atoms here
    Explanation: In ionic bonding, one atom (usually a metal) transfers one or more electrons to another atom (usually a nonmetal), creating positively and negatively charged ions.

  Q4. In a covalent bond, what happens to the electrons?
    [ ] Electrons are transferred from metal to nonmetal
    [*] Electrons are shared between the two bonded atoms
    [ ] Electrons are removed from both atoms completely
    [ ] Electrons are absorbed into the nucleus of one atom
    Explanation: In covalent bonding, atoms share one or more pairs of electrons. Both atoms contribute electrons to the shared pair, and both atoms count the shared electrons as part of their outer shell.

  Q5. Which of the following is an example of an ionic compound?
    [ ] Water (H2O)
    [*] Sodium chloride (NaCl)
    [ ] Carbon dioxide (CO2)
    [ ] Methane (CH4)
    Explanation: Sodium chloride (NaCl) is ionic because sodium is a metal that transfers its electron to chlorine, a nonmetal. The other compounds listed are covalent because they involve only nonmetals.

  Q6. Do ionic compounds generally have high or low melting points?
    [ ] Low melting points compared to most substances
    [*] High melting points compared to most substances
    [ ] They have no definite melting point at all
    [ ] The same melting points as covalent compounds
    Explanation: Ionic compounds generally have high melting points because the strong electrostatic attraction between positive and negative ions requires a lot of energy to overcome.

  Q7. Can ionic compounds conduct electricity when dissolved in water?
    [ ] No, they cannot conduct electricity under any conditions
    [*] Yes, because the ions are free to move and carry charge
    [ ] Only if they are heated above their boiling point first
    [ ] Only if they are mixed with covalent compounds in water
    Explanation: When dissolved in water, ionic compounds dissociate into free-moving ions that can carry electrical current. In solid form, the ions are locked in place and cannot conduct.

  Q8. Do most covalent compounds conduct electricity?
    [ ] Yes, covalent compounds are excellent conductors always
    [*] No, most covalent compounds do not conduct electricity
    [ ] Only when they are in solid form will they conduct well
    [ ] Only covalent gases can conduct electricity when heated
    Explanation: Most covalent compounds do not conduct electricity because they consist of neutral molecules without free-moving charged particles. There are no ions to carry electrical current.

  Q9. Ionic compounds form organized structures called what?
    [*] Crystal lattices
    [ ] Molecular chains
    [ ] Electron clouds
    [ ] Covalent networks
    Explanation: Ionic compounds form crystal lattices -- organized, repeating three-dimensional arrangements of alternating positive and negative ions. This structure gives ionic compounds their characteristic properties.

  Q10. Are covalent compounds generally solid, liquid, or gas at room temperature?
    [ ] Always solid at room temperature
    [ ] Always liquid at room temperature
    [*] They can be solid, liquid, or gas
    [ ] Always gas at room temperature only
    Explanation: Covalent compounds can be found in any state at room temperature. Water is liquid, sugar is solid, and carbon dioxide is a gas. Their lower intermolecular forces allow for this variety.

  Q11. What is an ionic bond?
    [*] A bond formed by the transfer of electrons from one atom to another creating ions
    [ ] A bond formed by the sharing of electrons between two nonmetal atoms in a pair
    [ ] A bond formed by the attraction between positive metal ions and free electrons
    [ ] A weak bond that exists between molecules rather than within individual molecules
    Explanation: An ionic bond is the electrostatic attraction between oppositely charged ions formed when one atom transfers electrons to another. Typically, a metal loses electrons and a nonmetal gains them.

  Q12. What is a covalent bond?
    [*] A bond formed when two atoms share one or more pairs of electrons between them
    [ ] A bond formed when one atom transfers all its electrons to another atom nearby
    [ ] A bond that holds metal atoms together through a sea of shared free electrons
    [ ] A bond formed by the magnetic attraction between two atoms placed near each other
    Explanation: A covalent bond forms when two atoms share one or more pairs of electrons. Both atoms contribute electrons to the bond, and the shared electrons are attracted to both nuclei.

  Q13. What does 'electrostatic attraction' mean?
    [*] The attractive force between oppositely charged particles like ions
    [ ] The repulsive force between particles that have the same charge
    [ ] The force of gravity that pulls all atoms toward Earth's center
    [ ] The magnetic force that exists between iron atoms in a magnet
    Explanation: Electrostatic attraction is the force that draws oppositely charged particles together. In ionic bonds, positive cations and negative anions are held together by this electrostatic attraction.

  Q14. What does 'crystal lattice' mean?
    [*] A regular repeating 3D arrangement of ions in an ionic compound
    [ ] A random arrangement of molecules in a covalent compound solid
    [ ] The pattern of electrons orbiting the nucleus of a single atom
    [ ] A type of laboratory equipment used to grow ionic compound crystals
    Explanation: A crystal lattice is the highly organized, three-dimensional repeating pattern in which ions are arranged in an ionic solid. Each positive ion is surrounded by negative ions and vice versa.

  Q15. What is a molecule?
    [*] A group of two or more atoms held together by covalent bonds
    [ ] A single atom that exists by itself without bonding to others
    [ ] Any substance that is found in the liquid state at room temp
    [ ] A pair of ions held together by electrostatic attraction force
    Explanation: A molecule is a group of two or more atoms held together by covalent bonds. Molecules are the smallest unit of a covalent compound that retains its chemical properties.

  Q16. What does 'formula unit' mean for ionic compounds?
    [*] The simplest ratio of ions in an ionic compound like NaCl or MgO
    [ ] A single molecule of an ionic compound floating freely in solution
    [ ] The total number of atoms present in one crystal of the compound
    [ ] The equipment formula used to measure ionic compounds in the lab
    Explanation: A formula unit is the simplest ratio of ions in an ionic compound. NaCl has a 1:1 ratio of Na+ to Cl-. Ionic compounds do not form discrete molecules but rather extended crystal lattices.

  Q17. What does 'electron transfer' mean?
    [*] When one atom gives up electrons to another atom during ionic bonding
    [ ] When two atoms equally share their electrons in a covalent bond made
    [ ] When electrons move from one energy level to another inside one atom
    [ ] When electricity flows through a wire made of a conducting metal material
    Explanation: Electron transfer is when one or more electrons move completely from one atom to another during ionic bond formation, creating a cation (positive ion) and an anion (negative ion).

  Q18. What does 'electron sharing' mean?
    [*] When two atoms both contribute electrons to a bond and share them
    [ ] When one atom takes all the electrons from the other atom entirely
    [ ] When electrons are evenly distributed across all atoms in a sample
    [ ] When electrons leave both atoms and exist freely between molecules
    Explanation: Electron sharing occurs in covalent bonds where two atoms both contribute electrons to a shared pair. The shared electrons are attracted to the nuclei of both atoms.

  Q19. What is a polyatomic ion?
    [*] A charged group of covalently bonded atoms that acts as a single unit
    [ ] A single atom that has gained or lost electrons to form a charged ion
    [ ] A neutral molecule made up of three or more different types of atoms
    [ ] Two ions that are bonded together forming a simple ionic compound pair
    Explanation: A polyatomic ion is a group of atoms that are covalently bonded together but carry an overall charge. Examples include hydroxide (OH-), sulfate (SO4 2-), and ammonium (NH4+).

  Q20. What does 'dissociation' mean for ionic compounds?
    [*] The separation of an ionic compound into individual ions when dissolved in water
    [ ] The formation of an ionic compound from individual metal and nonmetal elements
    [ ] The breaking of covalent bonds within a molecule to form separate free atoms
    [ ] The evaporation of an ionic compound from solid state directly into gas phase
    Explanation: Dissociation is when an ionic compound separates into its individual ions when dissolved in water. For example, NaCl dissociates into Na+ and Cl- ions in solution.

  Q21. Table salt (NaCl) has a melting point of 801 degrees Celsius, while sugar (C12H22O11) melts at 186 degrees Celsius. What explains this large difference?
    [*] Salt is ionic with strong ion attractions while sugar is covalent with weaker forces
    [ ] Salt has more atoms per molecule than sugar so it takes more energy to melt it
    [ ] Sugar is a metal compound while salt is a nonmetal compound making them different
    [ ] Salt is heavier than sugar so heavier substances always have higher melting points
    Explanation: NaCl is an ionic compound with strong electrostatic attractions in its crystal lattice, requiring high temperatures to break apart. Sugar is a covalent compound with weaker intermolecular forces.

  Q22. A student dissolves salt in water and tests it with a light bulb conductivity tester. The light turns on. Then the student dissolves sugar in water and the light does not turn on. Why?
    [*] Salt water conducts because dissolved ions carry charge while sugar has no ions
    [ ] Sugar water is too thick for electricity to pass through while salt water is thin
    [ ] The light bulb was broken during the second test with the sugar water solution
    [ ] Salt produces heat when dissolved which powers the light while sugar does not
    Explanation: Salt (NaCl) is ionic and dissociates into Na+ and Cl- ions in water, which carry electrical current. Sugar is covalent and dissolves as neutral molecules, so it cannot conduct electricity.

  Q23. Carbon dioxide (CO2) is a covalent compound that is a gas at room temperature. Calcium oxide (CaO) is an ionic compound that is a solid with a very high melting point. Both contain oxygen. What causes their different properties?
    [ ] They contain different types of oxygen atoms that have different properties
    [*] CO2 has covalent bonds with weak intermolecular forces while CaO has strong ionic bonds
    [ ] CO2 is larger than CaO so it cannot hold together as a solid at room temperature
    [ ] Calcium is heavier than carbon which automatically makes CaO a solid at room temperature
    Explanation: CO2 consists of individual molecules held together by weak intermolecular forces (gas at room temperature). CaO has strong ionic bonds in a crystal lattice requiring enormous energy to break.

  Q24. Magnesium and chlorine react to form MgCl2. Magnesium has 2 valence electrons and chlorine has 7. How does the electron transfer work?
    [ ] Magnesium transfers 1 electron to one chlorine atom only in the compound
    [ ] Each chlorine atom transfers 1 electron to magnesium in the reaction here
    [*] Magnesium transfers 1 electron to each of 2 chlorine atoms in the compound
    [ ] Magnesium and chlorine share electrons equally forming a covalent bond pair
    Explanation: Magnesium loses its 2 valence electrons, giving 1 to each of 2 chlorine atoms. Magnesium becomes Mg2+ and each chlorine becomes Cl-. This creates the ionic compound MgCl2.

  Q25. A student is given an unknown white solid. It dissolves in water and the solution conducts electricity. It also has a high melting point. Is it likely ionic or covalent?
    [ ] Covalent, because it dissolves in water and is a white solid substance
    [*] Ionic, because it conducts when dissolved and has a high melting point
    [ ] Neither, because only metals conduct electricity in water solutions here
    [ ] Both, because all compounds are both ionic and covalent at the same time
    Explanation: Conducting electricity when dissolved in water and having a high melting point are both characteristic of ionic compounds. The ions dissociate in water and carry charge.

  Q26. A student asks why water (a covalent compound) is liquid at room temperature while many other covalent compounds are gases. What is the best explanation?
    [*] Water has strong hydrogen bonds between its molecules keeping it liquid
    [ ] Water is actually an ionic compound which is why it is liquid at room temp
    [ ] Water molecules are heavier than all other covalent compounds' molecules
    [ ] Water has metallic bonds that make it a liquid at standard room temperature
    Explanation: Water is liquid at room temperature because of strong hydrogen bonds between water molecules. These intermolecular forces are stronger than those in most covalent compounds, keeping water in the liquid state.

  Q27. Sodium has 1 valence electron and oxygen has 6. When they form an ionic compound, what is the correct formula?
    [ ] NaO, because one sodium bonds with one oxygen
    [*] Na2O, because two sodiums each give one electron to oxygen
    [ ] NaO2, because sodium needs two oxygens to be stable
    [ ] Na6O, because oxygen has 6 valence electrons to fill
    Explanation: Oxygen needs 2 electrons to complete its octet, but each sodium can only give 1. So 2 sodium atoms each transfer 1 electron to oxygen, making the formula Na2O.

  Q28. A student holds an ice cube (solid water) and a salt crystal. Both are solid, but the student knows they are very different types of compounds. How could the student test which is ionic?
    [*] Dissolve each in water and test if the solution conducts electricity with a circuit
    [ ] Weigh each sample because ionic compounds are always heavier than covalent ones
    [ ] Look at the color because ionic compounds are always white and covalent are clear
    [ ] Smell each sample because ionic compounds have strong odors and covalent do not
    Explanation: Dissolving in water and testing conductivity is a reliable way to distinguish ionic from covalent compounds. The salt solution will conduct electricity (ions present) while sugar water will not.

  Q29. Calcium fluoride (CaF2) is used in toothpaste. Calcium is a metal with 2 valence electrons and fluorine is a nonmetal with 7. What type of bonding holds this compound together?
    [ ] Covalent bonding because both elements need electrons for their outer shell
    [ ] Metallic bonding because calcium is a metal in the alkaline earth group
    [*] Ionic bonding because a metal transfers electrons to nonmetal atoms here
    [ ] Hydrogen bonding because fluorine is involved and it bonds with hydrogen
    Explanation: CaF2 is held together by ionic bonds. Calcium (metal) transfers its 2 valence electrons, one to each of 2 fluorine atoms (nonmetals), forming Ca2+ and 2 F- ions.

  Q30. Diamond is made entirely of carbon atoms bonded together. Is diamond an ionic or covalent substance, and what property gives evidence for your answer?
    [ ] Ionic, because diamond is very hard and only ionic compounds can be hard
    [*] Covalent, because carbon atoms share electrons and diamond does not conduct
    [ ] Ionic, because diamond has a very high melting point like all ionic substances
    [ ] Neither, because diamond is an element and only compounds can have bonds
    Explanation: Diamond is a covalent network solid. Carbon atoms share electrons with each other (covalent bonds). Evidence includes that diamond does not conduct electricity, which is typical of covalent substances.

--- Lesson 3.21: Polar & Nonpolar Molecules (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Polar & Nonpolar Molecules
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
Oil does not dissolve in water. What is the best explanation for this?
Oil is nonpolar while water is polar. Since 'like dissolves like,' polar water molecules are attracted to each other and exclude the nonpolar oil molecules, causing them to separate.
What is a dipole?
A dipole is a separation of electrical charge, where one end of a molecule or bond has a partial positive charge and the other end has a partial negative charge.
What is electronegativity?
Electronegativity is a measure of how strongly an atom attracts the shared electrons in a chemical bond. Atoms with higher electronegativity pull electrons closer to themselves.
What is a nonpolar covalent bond?
A nonpolar covalent bond occurs when two atoms share electrons equally, typically because they have the same or very similar electronegativities. Examples include H-H in H2 and O=O in O2.
What is a polar covalent bond?
A polar covalent bond forms when two atoms share electrons unequally because one atom has a higher electronegativity than the other. The shared electrons spend more time near the more electronegative atom.
What is a solvent?
A solvent is the substance that does the dissolving in a solution. Water is called the 'universal solvent' because it can dissolve many different polar and ionic substances.
Two molecules both contain polar bonds, but Molecule A is nonpolar overall while Molecule B is polar overall. What is the most likely explanation?
Molecular shape determines whether polar bonds cancel or add up. In Molecule A, the symmetrical arrangement causes the bond dipoles to cancel, making it nonpolar overall. Molecule B's shape leaves a net dipole.

--- Lesson 3.21: Polar & Nonpolar Molecules (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What determines whether a molecule is polar or nonpolar?
    [ ] The total number of atoms in the molecule
    [*] The shape of the molecule and the difference in electronegativity between atoms
    [ ] The color and physical state of the substance at room temperature
    [ ] The atomic number of the largest atom present in the molecule
    Explanation: A molecule's polarity depends on both its shape (geometry) and the electronegativity differences between bonded atoms. Even if bonds are polar, a symmetrical shape can make the overall molecule nonpolar.

  Q2. Which of the following is a nonpolar molecule?
    [*] Carbon dioxide (CO2)
    [ ] Hydrogen fluoride (HF)
    [ ] Water (H2O) molecules
    [ ] Ammonia (NH3) gas
    Explanation: CO2 is nonpolar because it has a linear, symmetrical shape. The two polar C=O bonds point in opposite directions and cancel each other out, resulting in no net dipole moment.

  Q3. Why is water (H2O) considered a polar molecule?
    [ ] Because it contains only hydrogen and oxygen atoms in its structure
    [ ] Because it is a liquid at standard room temperature and pressure
    [*] Because its bent shape causes an uneven distribution of electrical charge
    [ ] Because oxygen is the most abundant element found on planet Earth
    Explanation: Water is polar because its bent shape prevents the two O-H bond dipoles from canceling out. Oxygen pulls electrons more strongly, creating a partial negative charge on the oxygen side and partial positive charges on the hydrogen side.

  Q4. What happens when polar and nonpolar substances are mixed together?
    [ ] They always react chemically to form brand new compounds
    [*] They generally do not mix well and tend to separate from each other
    [ ] They always form a perfectly uniform and homogeneous mixture
    [ ] They always produce a gas as a result of being combined
    Explanation: The general rule is 'like dissolves like.' Polar substances dissolve well in other polar substances, and nonpolar substances dissolve well in other nonpolar substances, but polar and nonpolar substances tend to separate.

  Q5. Which phrase best describes a nonpolar molecule?
    [ ] A molecule that has an unequal distribution of charge across its structure
    [ ] A molecule that always exists as a solid at standard room temperature
    [*] A molecule where charges are distributed evenly with no positive or negative end
    [ ] A molecule made up of only one single type of atom bonded together
    Explanation: In a nonpolar molecule, electrons are shared equally or the molecular shape causes bond dipoles to cancel, so there is no net positive or negative end.

  Q6. Oil does not dissolve in water. What is the best explanation for this?
    [ ] Oil is too heavy to dissolve in water so it sinks to the bottom of the container
    [*] Oil is nonpolar and water is polar, so they do not mix well with each other
    [ ] Oil and water are both polar substances that actively repel one another
    [ ] Water molecules are much larger than the oil molecules surrounding them
    Explanation: Oil is nonpolar while water is polar. Since 'like dissolves like,' polar water molecules are attracted to each other and exclude the nonpolar oil molecules, causing them to separate.

  Q7. Which molecule has a symmetrical shape that makes it nonpolar despite having polar bonds?
    [ ] Water (H2O), which has a bent molecular shape
    [ ] Ammonia (NH3), which has a pyramidal shape
    [*] Methane (CH4), which has a tetrahedral shape
    [ ] Hydrogen fluoride (HF), a diatomic molecule
    Explanation: Methane (CH4) has four polar C-H bonds arranged in a perfectly symmetrical tetrahedral shape. The bond dipoles cancel each other out evenly, making the overall molecule nonpolar.

  Q8. What type of bond exists between two atoms that share electrons equally?
    [ ] An ionic bond between a metal and a nonmetal element
    [ ] A polar covalent bond between atoms of different elements
    [*] A nonpolar covalent bond between atoms with similar electronegativities
    [ ] A metallic bond found in a pure sample of metal atoms
    Explanation: When two atoms have the same or very similar electronegativities, they share electrons equally, forming a nonpolar covalent bond. This commonly occurs between two atoms of the same element, like H2 or O2.

  Q9. Which property is commonly associated with polar molecules?
    [ ] They are always found in the gaseous state at room temperature
    [*] They tend to dissolve easily in water and other polar solvents
    [ ] They are unable to form any type of intermolecular attraction
    [ ] They always contain at least one metallic element in their formula
    Explanation: Polar molecules dissolve well in polar solvents like water because the partial charges on polar molecules are attracted to the partial charges on the solvent molecules, following the 'like dissolves like' rule.

  Q10. In a polar molecule, where do electrons tend to spend more of their time?
    [ ] They spend equal time near every atom in the molecule without preference
    [ ] They orbit far away from all the atoms within the molecular structure
    [ ] They cluster only at the geometric center of the molecular structure
    [*] They spend more time closer to the atom with higher electronegativity
    Explanation: In a polar molecule, the more electronegative atom pulls shared electrons closer to itself. This creates a partial negative charge near that atom and a partial positive charge on the other side.

  Q11. What is a dipole?
    [*] A separation of positive and negative charges within a molecule or bond
    [ ] A type of chemical reaction between two different polar substances
    [ ] A measurement of how fast molecules move through a solution or gas
    [ ] A pair of atoms that are bonded together by sharing three electrons
    Explanation: A dipole is a separation of electrical charge, where one end of a molecule or bond has a partial positive charge and the other end has a partial negative charge.

  Q12. What does the term 'polar' mean when describing a molecule?
    [ ] The molecule is made entirely of metallic elements bonded together
    [*] The molecule has an uneven charge distribution with distinct positive and negative ends
    [ ] The molecule can exist in both a solid and a liquid state simultaneously
    [ ] The molecule is extremely cold and only found near the Earth's polar regions
    Explanation: A polar molecule has an uneven distribution of electron density, giving it a partial positive end and a partial negative end, similar to the two poles of a magnet.

  Q13. What is electronegativity?
    [ ] The total number of electrons present in an atom's outermost energy level
    [ ] The speed at which electrons travel around the nucleus of a given atom
    [*] An atom's ability to attract shared electrons toward itself in a chemical bond
    [ ] The amount of energy required to completely remove an electron from an atom
    Explanation: Electronegativity is a measure of how strongly an atom attracts the shared electrons in a chemical bond. Atoms with higher electronegativity pull electrons closer to themselves.

  Q14. What does 'like dissolves like' mean in chemistry?
    [ ] Substances with similar molecular weights always dissolve in each other easily
    [*] Polar substances dissolve best in polar solvents and nonpolar in nonpolar solvents
    [ ] Any two liquids placed in the same container will eventually dissolve together
    [ ] Solids only dissolve in liquids that have exactly the same chemical formula
    Explanation: 'Like dissolves like' means that substances with similar polarity tend to dissolve in each other. Polar solvents dissolve polar solutes, and nonpolar solvents dissolve nonpolar solutes.

  Q15. What is a nonpolar covalent bond?
    [ ] A bond formed when electrons are completely transferred from one atom to another
    [ ] A bond where two atoms share electrons unequally due to differing electronegativities
    [*] A bond where two atoms share electrons equally because they have similar electronegativities
    [ ] A bond that only forms between metals and nonmetals in an ionic crystal lattice
    Explanation: A nonpolar covalent bond occurs when two atoms share electrons equally, typically because they have the same or very similar electronegativities. Examples include H-H in H2 and O=O in O2.

  Q16. What does 'molecular geometry' mean?
    [ ] The weight of a single molecule measured in atomic mass units
    [*] The three-dimensional arrangement of atoms within a molecule
    [ ] The temperature at which a molecule changes from liquid to gas
    [ ] The total number of chemical bonds present inside a molecule
    Explanation: Molecular geometry refers to the three-dimensional shape formed by the arrangement of atoms in a molecule. Common shapes include linear, bent, trigonal planar, and tetrahedral.

  Q17. What is a polar covalent bond?
    [*] A bond where atoms share electrons unequally due to a difference in electronegativity
    [ ] A bond where electrons are completely transferred from one atom to another atom
    [ ] A bond that forms only between two identical atoms of the same exact element
    [ ] A bond that requires extremely high temperatures in order to form properly
    Explanation: A polar covalent bond forms when two atoms share electrons unequally because one atom has a higher electronegativity than the other. The shared electrons spend more time near the more electronegative atom.

  Q18. What does 'partial charge' mean in the context of polar molecules?
    [*] A small amount of positive or negative charge that develops on part of a polar molecule
    [ ] The full positive charge carried by a proton inside the nucleus of every atom
    [ ] An electrical charge that only appears when a molecule is heated above boiling point
    [ ] The charge of an electron that has been completely removed from a neutral atom
    Explanation: A partial charge is a slight positive or negative charge on part of a molecule. It occurs because electrons are not shared equally in polar bonds, written as delta-plus and delta-minus symbols.

  Q19. What is a solvent?
    [ ] A substance that is being dissolved by another substance in a solution
    [*] A substance that dissolves another substance to form a uniform solution
    [ ] A chemical reaction that produces heat energy as one of its products
    [ ] A solid substance that cannot be dissolved in any type of liquid
    Explanation: A solvent is the substance that does the dissolving in a solution. Water is called the 'universal solvent' because it can dissolve many different polar and ionic substances.

  Q20. What does 'miscible' mean?
    [*] Two liquids that are able to mix together in any proportion to form a single layer
    [ ] Two gases that react violently whenever they come into contact with each other
    [ ] A solid that can only dissolve in water at very high temperatures and pressures
    [ ] A substance that changes color whenever it is placed into a different solution
    Explanation: Miscible means two liquids can mix together completely in any proportion. For example, ethanol and water are miscible because they are both polar and mix in all ratios.

  Q21. A student tries to clean grease off their hands using only water, but the grease does not wash away easily. Why does this happen?
    [ ] Water is too cold to dissolve grease under normal conditions in a classroom
    [*] Grease is nonpolar while water is polar, so they do not dissolve each other
    [ ] Water molecules are too large to fit between the tiny grease molecules present
    [ ] Grease has a much higher density than water causing it to stay on skin surfaces
    Explanation: Grease is made of nonpolar hydrocarbon molecules, while water is polar. Since 'like dissolves like,' the polar water cannot effectively dissolve the nonpolar grease. Soap works because it has both polar and nonpolar parts.

  Q22. In a lab, a student observes that sugar dissolves easily in water but not in vegetable oil. What explains this observation?
    [ ] Sugar crystals are physically too large to fit between the oil molecule chains
    [*] Sugar is polar and dissolves in polar water, but not in the nonpolar vegetable oil
    [ ] Oil is heavier than sugar, which prevents the sugar from sinking and dissolving
    [ ] Sugar must be heated above 200 degrees before it can dissolve in any oil substance
    Explanation: Sugar is a polar molecule with many O-H groups that can interact with polar water molecules. Vegetable oil is nonpolar, so it cannot form attractions with the polar sugar molecules.

  Q23. A student notices that a charged balloon can deflect a thin stream of water but not a stream of hexane. What does this tell you about these liquids?
    [ ] Water is heavier than hexane, so the balloon's static charge affects it much more
    [*] Water is polar and attracted to the charge, while hexane is nonpolar and is not attracted
    [ ] Hexane is magnetic and repels the charged balloon while water molecules are not magnetic
    [ ] The balloon only works on liquids that have a temperature below twenty degrees Celsius
    Explanation: Polar molecules like water have partial charges that are attracted to the charged balloon, causing the stream to bend. Nonpolar hexane molecules have no significant partial charges, so they are not deflected.

  Q24. A nail polish remover dissolves nail polish quickly but does not mix well with water. Based on this, what can you conclude about nail polish and the remover?
    [*] Both the nail polish and remover are likely nonpolar substances that dissolve each other
    [ ] Both the nail polish and remover are polar substances similar in nature to water
    [ ] Nail polish is polar and the remover is nonpolar, creating a strong chemical reaction
    [ ] The remover works by heating the nail polish until it evaporates from the surface
    Explanation: Since the remover dissolves nail polish but does not mix with polar water, both the nail polish and remover must be nonpolar. This follows the 'like dissolves like' principle.

  Q25. A student mixes rubbing alcohol with water and they form a single uniform layer. Then the student tries mixing cooking oil with water and they form two separate layers. What explains the difference?
    [ ] Rubbing alcohol is lighter than cooking oil so it mixes better with water overall
    [ ] Cooking oil has larger molecules that physically cannot fit between water molecules
    [*] Rubbing alcohol is polar like water so they mix, but oil is nonpolar so it separates
    [ ] Water can only dissolve liquids that are clear and transparent in their appearance
    Explanation: Rubbing alcohol has polar O-H groups that allow it to form attractions with polar water molecules, making them miscible. Cooking oil is nonpolar and cannot form these attractions with water, so they separate.

  Q26. A scientist is choosing a solvent to dissolve a nonpolar wax. Which solvent would work best?
    [ ] Pure distilled water, which is a well-known universal solvent for many substances
    [ ] A saltwater solution, since salt helps break down many types of organic materials
    [ ] Vinegar, which is a polar liquid made of acetic acid dissolved in water molecules
    [*] Turpentine, which is a nonpolar organic solvent commonly used in paint products
    Explanation: Since wax is nonpolar, the best solvent is another nonpolar substance. Turpentine is a nonpolar organic solvent that can dissolve nonpolar wax effectively, following the 'like dissolves like' rule.

  Q27. Two molecules both contain polar bonds, but Molecule A is nonpolar overall while Molecule B is polar overall. What is the most likely explanation?
    [*] Molecule A has a symmetrical shape that cancels out its bond dipoles evenly
    [ ] Molecule A contains more total atoms than Molecule B does in its structure
    [ ] Molecule B has weaker bonds that make it more polar than Molecule A overall
    [ ] Molecule A is in a gaseous state while Molecule B is currently a liquid
    Explanation: Molecular shape determines whether polar bonds cancel or add up. In Molecule A, the symmetrical arrangement causes the bond dipoles to cancel, making it nonpolar overall. Molecule B's shape leaves a net dipole.

  Q28. A student is painting with watercolors. The paint dissolves easily in water but not in baby oil. The student then tries oil paint, which dissolves in baby oil but not in water. What can the student conclude?
    [*] Watercolor paint is polar and oil paint is nonpolar, matching their respective solvents
    [ ] Both types of paint are actually polar but they simply react differently with oils
    [ ] Baby oil has a higher temperature than water which is why it dissolves oil paint
    [ ] Watercolor paint and oil paint have exactly the same polarity and chemical makeup
    Explanation: Watercolor paint is polar (dissolves in polar water), while oil paint is nonpolar (dissolves in nonpolar baby oil). Each paint dissolves in a solvent that matches its polarity.

  Q29. During a lab experiment, a student adds food coloring to water and it spreads evenly. When the student adds the same food coloring to cooking oil, it forms droplets that do not spread. What does this demonstrate?
    [ ] Food coloring is denser than oil, so it naturally sinks and forms round droplets
    [ ] Cooking oil is thicker than water, which physically prevents the color from moving
    [*] Food coloring is polar so it mixes with polar water but not with nonpolar cooking oil
    [ ] The food coloring reacts chemically with water but does not react with cooking oil
    Explanation: Food coloring is a water-based polar substance. It dissolves in polar water because the molecules attract each other. In nonpolar cooking oil, the polar food coloring molecules clump together instead of mixing.

  Q30. A dry cleaning company uses a nonpolar chemical solvent instead of water to clean delicate clothes. Why would a nonpolar solvent be more effective for removing certain stains?
    [ ] Nonpolar solvents are always stronger than water at removing every type of stain
    [ ] Nonpolar solvents are cheaper than water so dry cleaners use them to save money
    [*] Many clothing stains come from nonpolar substances like oils and greases that water cannot dissolve
    [ ] Nonpolar solvents evaporate instantly which prevents any possible damage to the fabrics
    Explanation: Many stains on clothing come from nonpolar substances like body oils, grease, and cosmetics. Since water is polar and cannot dissolve these nonpolar stains, a nonpolar solvent is needed to dissolve and remove them.
